Amongst the Indian rhetoricians Rudra·π≠a occupies a prominent place both by his age and the value of his works. Of these two are known, the KaÃÇvyaÃÇla·πÅkaÃÇraand the √á·πõ·πÖgaÃÇratilaka, and as all the quotations from Rudra·π≠a I have come upon, with few exceptions probably due to an inadvertence of the writers, are actually found in either of these two, Rudra·π≠a perhaps did not compose any other work.

A shorter form of the name Rudra·π≠a is Rudra, and a longer one Rudrabha·π≠·π≠a, Rudrabha·π≠a or Bha·π≠·π≠arudra¬π), and since the names Rudra and Rudrabha·π≠·π≠a are frequently met with in Indian literature, it is at present impossible to make out with certainty the exact number of works of the famous rhetorician. After examining, however, the numerous Reports, Catalogues and Lists of Sanskrit MSS. accessible to me, I am of opinion, that of all the Rudras and Rudrabha·π≠·π≠as hitherto known, there is none who with any probability might be identified with our author.

is made with a verbal form‚Äú. (227) The mutual relation of a thing compared and another thing with which it is compared, to be possible, requires that both should have some property in common. But in the present instance, what common property can you discern in ‚Äûanoints‚Äú and ‚Äûdarkness‚Äú? (228) If you maintain, that ‚Äûanointing‚Äú itself is that property, then what in the world is ‚Äûanoints‚Äú besides? Surely even a madman would not say that one and the same thing is property and possessor of such property at the same time. (229) If you say that it is the agent (denoted by the termination of limpati) with whom darkness is compared, (I rejoin, that) that agent, having become subordinate to the action denoted by the verbal base, is altogether occupied in bringing about that action and can therefore enter into no relation with anything else. (230) When on the other hand you say, that darkness is like one who anoints, then‚Äû the limbs‚Äú cannot be taken as the object of the subject ‚Äûdarkness‚Äú and besides the quality common to both has still to be pointed out. (231) When we say: ‚Äû Your face is like the moon‚Äú, we understand (something more than what is actually said, viz. we understand) that the face is lovely. Such is not the case here; for here we understand from ‚Äûanoints‚Äú nothing else but , anoints‚Äú. (232) The (rhetorical figure) utprek·π£aÃÇ is indicated by the words manye (I think), √ßa·πÖke (I suspect), dhruvam (certainly), prayas (generally), n√ªnam (surely) and similar words; the word iva is also such a one‚Äú. (234)

From this it is obvious, that Da·πá·∏çin is of opinion that the word iva is also indicative of the rhetorical figure utprek·π£aÃÇ, a fact, which apparently had been disputed by other rhetoricians. Of these MSS. ADE and BC may be classed together. ADE with few exceptions do not give the headings before the single rules and illustrations, which are, again with a few exceptions, not numbered in AD. Whether these headings are Rudra·π≠a’s is questionable. Since traces of them are found in all MSS. and since in some cases they are absolutely necessary for perspicuity’s sake, I have thought it advisable to follow the MSS. BC. MS. A contains all the peculiarities of the √á√¢rad√¢-MSS. Instead of Visarga it writes √ß before √ß and s before s, the Jihv√¢m√ªl√Æya before gutturals, the Upadhm√¢n√Æya before labials ¬π). I have not followed it in this, though on the last two sounds my view is very different from Whitney’s (Sanskrit Grammar ¬ß 69). For want of types I have been obliged to quote A’s readings even in the critical notes in the usually adopted method of writing without the Upadhm√¢n√Æya. B. ‚ÄîA recent Devan√¢gar√Æ MS. copied incorrectly from a good archetype in √á√¢rad√¢-characters, B√ºbler 1. c. No. 266. foll. 3. Lines on a page 12. Size of leaves 13¬Ω √ó 6.

The numbering of the single rules, if rules they are to be called, is my own. The MSS. give a running text.

In the Notes I have taken care to give the quotations of Rudra·π≠a’s illustrations as completely as possible. As to the rules, I have confined the references to printed works.

I have to tender my sincere acknowledgments to Professor F. Kielhorn for the kind help which he has given me by going through this Introduction and correcting it whereever this was necessary; nor would I conclude without expressing my thanks to my friend Mr. C. F. Haeseler for the liberality with which he has undertaken to publish this book.

Halle a/S., February 1886.

R. Pischel.

II. Notes on Ruyyaka.

1, 7. ‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§ö‡§ï‡§æ‡§ö‡•ç‡§Øperhaps means ‚Äûa string of glass-beads‚Äú‚Äî. 2, 2. Instead of ‡§™‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§™‡§∞‡§æ‡§ó all lexicographers and editors write ‡§™‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§™‡§∞‡§æ‡§ó and accordingly ‡§™‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§™‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú.
